The Faith of the Imitator: Consensus and Clarification
The commentator reports consensus on the imitator's faith, presents a fitra-based argument, and explains the tahqiq rendering the dispute verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Sunni consensus that the imitator is a believer, and how the dispute about his faith is ultimately a verbal disagreement.

Core Idea
The imitator's faith is valid by consensus; the disagreement is only verbal.

This passage brings together several key scholarly voices to settle a critical question: Is a person who believes in God without independent investigation (the muqallid) truly a believer? The text reports a strong consensus, addresses a scriptural argument from fitra, and finally shows how the apparent dispute reduces to a verbal one. By the end, you will see why the mainstream view affirms the imitator's faith and counts him among the people of Paradise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the text and the different opinions sink in before analyzing.
Notice the transition from reporting consensus to presenting a dissenting view, then to a scriptural argument, and finally to a resolution.
2
Identify the consensus and the lone dissenter
The passage begins by citing al-Amidi's report of agreement among scholars that the muqallid is not a disbeliever. The only exception mentioned is Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i. Pay attention to how the consensus is described.
This shows the strength of the majority position.
3
Examine the fitra-based argument and Maturidi's response
Some scholars (via Ibn Hajar) argued that knowledge of God is obtained by fitra, not by obligating reflection. They cite Q. 30:30 and the hadith. Then Maturidi affirms that common people are believers despite lacking technical expression.
Note how Maturidi uses fitra to explain why they are believers, not to negate the obligation of knowledge.
4
Understand the tahqiq that makes the dispute verbal
Taj al-Subki's clarification shows that the debate over whether imitation suffices is merely verbal—once the state of the muqallid is properly explained, the apparent disagreement disappears. The term 'tahqiq' here means 'mentioning a thing in its true manner'.
This is the key move: turning a seeming doctrinal dispute into a matter of wording.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider the practical impact: common Muslims who believe without detailed arguments are still believers and will be in Paradise. This upholds the mercy of God and the inclusiveness of faith.
This lesson reassures that faith does not require expertise in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكى الآمدي اتفاق الأصحاب على انتفاء كفر المقلّد، وأنه لا يُعرفُ القول بعدم صحة إيمانِه إلا لأبي هاشم الجبائي من المعتزلة . وذكر ابن حجر عن بعضهم : أنه أنكر وجوب المعرفة أصلاً، وقال: إنَّها حاصلة بأصل الفطرة، واستدل على ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿فِطْرَتَ اللَّهِ الَّتِي فَطَرَ النَّاسَ عَلَيْهَا الروم : ٣٠، وبقولِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم : كلُّ مولود يُولد على الفطرة» ، ولذلك قال أبو منصور الماتريدي: (أجمع أصحابنا على أن العوام مؤمنون عارفون يربِّهم، وأنهم حشو الجنة، كما جاءت به الأخبار، وانعقد به الإجماع؛ فإن فطرتهم جبلت على توحيد الصانع وقدمه وحدوث ما سواه، وإن عجزوا عن التعبير عنه بإصطلاح المتكلمين والله أعلم) . وقوله : (وَبَعْضُهُمْ حَقَّقَ فِيهِ الكَشْفَا ) أي : وبعض القوم كالتاج السبكي إيمان المقلّدِ البيان عن حاله بما يصيرُ به الخلاف في الاكتفاء بالتقليد وعدم الاكتفاء به لفظيا ، والتحقيق يطلق على ذكر الشيء على الوجه الحقِّ، وعلى إثبات الشيء بدليل، والأوَّلُ هو المراد هنا
And al-Amidi reported the agreement of the companions (scholars) on the negation of the kufr of the muqallid (imitator), and that the view of the invalidity of his faith is not known except from Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i of the Mu'tazila. And Ibn Hajar mentioned from some of them that he denied the obligation of knowledge (ma'rifa) entirely and said: 'It is obtained by the original nature (fiṭra),' and he argued for that by the saying of God Most High: 'The nature of God upon which He created mankind' [al-Rum: 30], and by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every child is born upon the fitra.' For this reason, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said: 'Our companions have agreed that the common people are believers, cognizant of their Lord, and that they are the filling of Paradise, as is reported in the traditions and consensus has been formed upon it. For their fitra has been created upon the tawhid of the Maker, His pre-eternity, and the temporal origination of everything else, even if they are unable to express it in the terminology of the theologians. And God knows best.' And his saying: 'And some of them achieved clarity (tahqiq) regarding it' means: And some of the people, like Taj al-Subki, brought forth a clarification (bayān) about the state of the imitator's faith such that the dispute concerning whether imitation suffices or not becomes merely verbal. And tahqiq is applied to mentioning a thing in its true manner, and to establishing a thing by evidence; the first meaning is intended here.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a synthesis of scholarly positions on the faith of the muqallid (the person who believes without independent reflection). It begins by citing al-Amidi's report of a consensus (ittifāq al-aṣḥāb) that the muqallid is not a kafir. The only dissenter mentioned is Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i of the Mu'tazila, who held that the imitator's faith is not valid. Then the commentator turns to a view from some scholars (via Ibn Hajar) who denied the obligation of theoretical knowledge (ma'rifa) altogether, arguing that such knowledge is obtained by the original human disposition (fiṭra). They supported this with Qur'an 30:30 and the hadith 'Every child is born upon the fitra.' In response, the commentator quotes Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who affirms that the common people are indeed believers and will fill Paradise, because their fitra is created upon the correct beliefs (tawhid, pre-eternity of God, temporal origination of the world), even if they cannot express these in technical theological terms. Finally, the commentator explains a line of the matn: 'And some of them achieved clarity (tahqiq) regarding it.' This refers to scholars like Taj al-Subki, who, by explaining the state of the imitator's faith, rendered the debate about whether imitation suffices a merely verbal dispute. The term 'tahqiq' here is defined: it means 'mentioning a thing in its true manner,' not 'establishing it by evidence.' Thus, the passage shows that the core consensus affirms the imitator's faith, and that any remaining disagreement is not substantive.

إيمان المقلد
الفطرة
التحقيق
خلاف لفظي

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The consensus is that the muqallid is a believer, and the only known dissenter is Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i.

Key Takeaway
The classical Sunni consensus affirms that the muqallid is a true believer and a resident of Paradise, and any disagreement about his faith is merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The scholars call this dispute 'verbal'—but your relationship with Allah is not verbal. Is your faith alive and active, or is it just a label you wear? If all your arguments were stripped away, would your heart still cling to its Lord with the same reliance?

The Faith of the Muqallid: Consensus and Innate Knowledge
Reports consensus on muqallid's faith and fitra argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari consensus on the validity of the muqallid's faith and the argument from innate disposition (fitra) as reported in the text.

Core Idea
The layperson's faith is valid by consensus and rooted in the fitra.

The status of a person who believes without detailed theological reasoning (the muqallid) has been a point of debate. This passage reports a strong Ash'ari consensus that such a person is not a disbeliever, and presents the view that knowledge of God is innate, supported by Qur'an and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two main parts: the consensus report and the fitra argument.
2
Identify the consensus and the dissenter
Find the statement of al-Amidi and note who the sole dissenter is (Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i). Understand that the Ash'aris agreed the muqallid is not a kafir.
3
Understand the fitra argument
Ibn Hajar reports a view that knowledge of God is obtained by fitra (innate disposition) and is not obligatory to acquire. Note the Quranic verse and hadith cited as evidence.
The text only reports this view, not necessarily endorses it as the only valid position.
4
Examine al-Maturidi's support
Read al-Maturidi's quote: he says the common people are believers and will be in Paradise because their fitra is created with tawhid, even if they cannot express it in technical terms.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this discussion matters: it defends the faith of ordinary Muslims and shows that theological expertise is not required for salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكى الآمدي اتفاق الأصحاب على انتفاء كفر المقلّد، وأنه لا يُعرفُ القول بعدم صحة إيمانِه إلا لأبي هاشم الجبائي من المعتزلة. وذكر ابن حجر عن بعضهم : أنه أنكر وجوب المعرفة أصلاً، وقال: إنَّها حاصلة بأصل الفطرة، واستدل على ذلك بقوله تعالى: {فِطْرَتَ اللَّهِ الَّتِي فَطَرَ النَّاسَ عَلَيْهَا} [الروم : 30]، وبقولِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «كلُّ مولود يُولد على الفطرة»، ولذلك قال أبو منصور الماتريدي: (أجمع أصحابنا على أن العوام مؤمنون عارفون يربهم، وأنهم حشو الجنة، كما جاءت به الأخبار، وانعقد به الإجماع؛ فإن فطرتهم جبلت على توحيد الصانع وقدمه وحدوث ما سواه، وإن عجزوا عن التعبير عنه باصطلاح المتكلمين والله أعلم).
Al-Amidi reported the agreement of the companions (al-ashab) on the negation of disbelief of the muqallid (the one who follows blindly), and that the opinion of the invalidity of his faith is not known except to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila. And Ibn Hajar mentioned from some of them: that he denied the obligation of knowledge (al-ma'rifa) entirely, and said: it is obtained by original disposition (fitra), and he adduced for that the saying of the Exalted: 'The fitra of Allah upon which He created mankind' [ar-Rum: 30], and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every child is born upon the fitra.' And therefore Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said: 'Our companions are agreed that the common people are believers, knowers of their Lord, and that they are the stuffing of Paradise, as the reports have come and consensus has been established upon it; for their fitra is created upon the tawhid of the Maker, His pre-eternity, and the temporal origination of what is besides Him, even if they are unable to express it in the terminology of the mutakallimun. And Allah knows best.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a report of historical opinions on the faith of the muqallid (the layperson who follows authority without independent investigation). It begins by citing al-Amidi's report of a consensus among the Ash'ari scholars that the muqallid is not a disbeliever, and that the only dissenter is Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila, who holds that the muqallid's faith is invalid. Then it mentions another view attributed to some individuals by Ibn Hajar: that knowledge of God is not obligatory at all because it is naturally present in every human by fitra (innate disposition). They support this with the Quranic verse 'the fitra of Allah' and the hadith 'every child is born upon the fitra.' Finally, it quotes Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who states that his school (the Maturidis) are agreed that the common people are true believers and will fill Paradise, because their fitra has been created with the knowledge of God's oneness, His pre-eternity, and the temporal nature of everything else, even if they cannot articulate it in technical kalam terms. Thus, the passage presents two arguments supporting the validity of the muqallid's faith: the consensus of the scholars and the fitra-based argument.

Key Terms

The Verbal Dispute over the Muqallid's Faith
Explains the tahqiq of the muqallid's faith and distinguishes verbal from substantive disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a verbal (lafzi) dispute and a substantive (ma'nawi) dispute in the context of the muqallid's faith, and why the first is considered merely verbal.

Core Idea
Not all disputes about faith are substantive; some are merely verbal.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid unpacks a subtle but important nuance in the debate about the faith of a muqallid—someone who follows without independent investigation. It shows how a key investigation (tahqiq) by scholars like Taj al-Subki transforms what appears to be a substantial disagreement into a verbal one, while another dispute remains substantive.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms: tahqiq, lafzi, ma'nawi.
The OCR has a slight issue with the phrase 'إيمان المقلّدِ البيان', but the meaning is clear.
2
Understand tahqiq in context
The passage defines tahqiq as 'mentioning a thing in its true manner' (not establishing by evidence). See how this applies to the muqallid's case.
This is a key distinction for the argument.
3
Identify the two disputes
Find where the passage mentions two different disputes: one between those who say the muqallid is a believer and those who say he is a disbeliever (verbal), and another among those who affirm his faith about his sinfulness (substantive).
Notice that one is lafzi, the other ma'nawi.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why it matters to label a dispute as verbal. It means the disagreement is only in wording, not in underlying belief. This can reduce theological tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (وَبَعْضُهُمْ حَقَّقَ فِيهِ الكَشْفَا ) أي : وبعض القوم كالتاج السبكي إيمان المقلّدِ البيان عن حاله بما يصيرُ به الخلاف في الاكتفاء بالتقليد وعدم الاكتفاء به لفظيا، والتحقيق يطلق على ذكر الشيء على الوجه الحقِّ، وعلى إثبات الشيء بدليل، والأوَّلُ هو المراد هنا. قوله : (بما يصير به الخلاف لفظيًّا ... إلى آخره) عُلم منه أن الخلاف الذي صار بهذا التحقيق = لفظيا ; هو الخلاف الواقع بين من قال بإيمانه ومن قال بكفره ; وأما الخلاف الواقع بين من قال بإيمانه، من كون المقلد مؤمناً عاصياً مطلقاً، أو غير عاص مطلقاً، أو فيه التفصيل، فهو معنوي عند البعض الثاني كما هو معنوي عند الأول. انتهى أجهوري.
And his saying: 'And some of them investigated (haqqaqa) therein the disclosure (al-kashf).' That is: And some of the people, like Taj al-Subki, [made] the faith of the muqallid the clarification (al-bayan) of his state such that the dispute concerning sufficiency of taqlid and its insufficiency becomes verbal (lafzi). And al-tahqiq is applied to mentioning a thing in the true manner, and to establishing a thing by evidence; the first is intended here. His saying: 'by which the dispute becomes verbal... to its end' — it is understood from it that the dispute that results from this investigation is verbal; it is the dispute occurring between one who affirms his faith and one who declares his disbelief. As for the dispute occurring between those who affirm his faith — whether the muqallid is a believer and a sinner absolutely, or not a sinner at all, or there is detail — it is substantive (ma'nawi) according to some, just as it is substantive according to the first. End of Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory gloss on the line 'And some of them investigated therein the disclosure.' It identifies 'some of them' as scholars like Taj al-Subki who provided a 'tahqiq' (investigation/verification) of the muqallid's faith. The tahqiq involves clarifying the muqallid's state such that the dispute over whether taqlid is sufficient for faith becomes merely verbal (lafzi) — that is, a disagreement in wording, not in substance. The term 'tahqiq' here is defined as 'mentioning a thing in its true manner' (not 'establishing by evidence'), which is the meaning intended. The passage then cites a clarification: the dispute that becomes verbal is the one between those who say the muqallid is a believer and those who say he is a disbeliever. However, within the group that affirms his faith, there is a further dispute: whether the muqallid is a sinner for omitting investigation, or not a sinner, or there is qualification. That dispute is substantive (ma'nawi), not merely verbal. Thus, the passage distinguishes between the verbal dispute over the muqallid's basic faith status and the substantive dispute over his moral culpability.

lafzi
ma'nawi
muqallid
Taj al-Subki

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All disagreements about the muqallid's faith are merely verbal.
Correction: Only the basic faith/disbelief dispute is verbal; the dispute about whether he is a sinner is substantive.

Checkpoint

A) Substantive (ma'nawi)
B) Verbal (lafzi)
C) Both
D) Unresolvable

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the muqallid's basic faith status is verbal, but the dispute over his sinfulness is substantive.

Conditional Sufficiency of Taqlid and Its Consequences
Commentator explains conditional sufficiency of taqlid and its limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition under which taqlid suffices for faith and the ruling of sin for neglecting nazar when capable.

Core Idea
Certainty in taqlid saves, but abandoning investigation when able is sinful.

In this passage, the commentator explains the conditional ruling on taqlid. He clarifies that if the muqallid holds strong certainty in the authority's statement, it is sufficient for his faith and worldly status. However, if he has the capacity to investigate (nazar) and neglects it, he is a sinner, though still a believer. This distinction is crucial for understanding the limits of taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the cut-off phrase 'fī al-ḍa…' — it likely means 'in error'.
2
Understand the condition of certainty
The passage defines 'jizm' as strong certainty so deep that the muqallid would not recant even if the authority recanted. This is the threshold for taqlid to suffice.
3
Grasp the sufficiency
When certain, the muqallid is treated as a full Muslim in worldly and eschatological rulings: marriage, inheritance, burial, salvation.
This shows social inclusion despite lack of direct investigation.
4
Identify the sin condition
The passage ends by warning: if the muqallid had capacity for nazar and abandoned it, he is a sinner (asi) even though his faith is valid.
This nuance is often overlooked.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify when the muqallid becomes a sinner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَقَالَ : إِنْ يَجْزِمُ بِقَوْلِ الغَيْرِ كَفَى وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَزَلْ فِي الضَّـ وقوله : (فَقَالَ ... إلخ)، معطوف على قوله : حقق فيه الكشفا من عطف المفصَّل على المجمل . وقوله : (إِنْ يَجْزِمْ بِقَوْلِ الغَيْرِ أي: إن يجزم المقلّد بصحة قول الغير جزماً قويًّا؛ بحيث لو رجع - بالفتح - لم يرجع المقلد - بالكسر :وقوله (كَفَى) أي: كفاه في الإيمان، وعلى هذا يحملُ القولُ بكفاية التقليد؛ فيكفيه ذلك في الأحكام الدنيوية؛ فيناكحُ، ويؤمُّ، وتُؤكل ذبيحته، ويرثُه المسلمون ويرتُهم، ويسهم له، ويدفن في مقابر المسلمين، وفي الأحكام الأخروية أيضاً، فلا يخلد في النار إن دخلها، وماله إلى النجاة والجنة، فهو مؤمن، لكنه عاص بترك النظر إن كان فيه أهلية للنظر.
He said: 'If he is certain of the statement of another, it suffices, and if not, he remains in [the]...' And his saying: 'He said... etc.' is conjunct to his saying 'haqqaqa fihi al-kashfa', it being the conjoining of the detailed to the summary. And his saying: 'If he is certain of the statement of another' means: if the muqallid is strongly certain of the correctness of the other's statement, such that if the authority (with fatha) recanted, the muqallid (with kasra) would not recant. And his saying: 'it suffices' means: it suffices him in faith, and upon this is carried the saying of sufficiency of taqlid. So it suffices him in worldly rulings: he may marry, lead prayer, his slaughtered meat is eaten, Muslims inherit from him and he inherits from them, he is given a share, and he is buried in Muslim cemeteries. And also in eschatological rulings: he will not abide forever in the Fire if he enters it, and his lot is salvation and Paradise. Thus he is a believer, but he is a sinner for abandoning investigation (nazar) if he had capacity for investigation.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line 'If he is certain of the statement of another, it suffices; otherwise he remains in [error].' The commentator first notes that this line is conjoined to the earlier phrase 'haqqaqa fihi al-kashfa' as a detailed explanation of that summary. Then he glosses 'if he is certain of the statement of another' as meaning that the muqallid has such strong certainty in the correctness of the authority's statement that even if the authority himself recanted, the muqallid would not recant. This indicates the depth of his conviction. The term 'kafa' (it suffices) is then explained: it suffices for faith in both worldly and religious rulings. In worldly matters, the muqallid is treated as a full Muslim: he can marry, lead prayer, his slaughtered meat is permissible, Muslims inherit from him, he inherits from them, he receives shares, and he is buried in Muslim cemeteries. In the afterlife, he will not be eternally in Hell if he commits sins; his ultimate fate is salvation and Paradise. However, the commentator adds a crucial qualification: the muqallid is a believer but he is a sinner (asi) for neglecting investigation (nazar) if he was capable of it. This means that taqlid is only excused for those incapable of independent reasoning; for those who are able, taqlid without investigation is sinful, though it does not negate their faith.

taqlid
nazar
asi
muqallid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that taqlid is always sufficient and the muqallid has no sin.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that sin applies if the muqallid had capacity for nazar but abandoned it.

D) If he follows a misguided authority

Key Takeaway
Taqlid suffices for faith only when accompanied by strong certainty, but if one can investigate independently, failure to do so incurs sin.

Condition for Sufficiency of Taqlid in Faith
Explains the condition of strong certainty for taqlid to suffice and distinguishes worldly and afterlife rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition of strong certainty (jazzm) required for taqlid to suffice in faith, and how this condition affects both worldly and afterlife rulings, as well as the nature of the dispute over the muqallid's iman.

Core Idea
Taqlid in faith requires unshakable certainty, not mere acceptance.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a critical condition for taqlid (imitation) to be sufficient for faith. The author explains that the muqallid (imitator) must have a strong, unwavering certainty in the truth of what he follows, to the point that he would not retract even if the imitated authority retracts. This condition determines whether taqlid suffices for both worldly and afterlife rulings, and it shapes the scholarly debate over whether the muqallid is a true believer or a disobedient sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the condition of jazzm
Find the phrase describing strong certainty (jazzm) and note how the author defines it: the muqallid must be so certain that he would not retract if the imitated person retracts.
This is the core condition for taqlid to be valid.
3
Distinguish worldly and afterlife rulings
Notice that taqlid with certainty suffices for both worldly matters (marriage, inheritance, etc.) and afterlife salvation, but the muqallid is still disobedient for not reflecting.
4
Understand the dispute
The passage explains that the disagreement over whether a muqallid is a believer or disbeliever is verbal, while the dispute over disobedience is substantive. This clarifies the nature of the scholarly debate.
5
Reflect on the consequence without certainty
Read the second part: if the muqallid lacks strong certainty, he remains in harm (dayr) because his faith is susceptible to doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَقَالَ : إِنْ يَجْزِمُ بِقَوْلِ الغَيْرِ كَفَى وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَزَلْ فِي الضَّيْرِ . وقوله : (فَقَالَ ... إلخ)، معطوف على قوله : حقق فيه الكشفا من عطف المفصَّل على المجمل . وقوله : (إِنْ يَجْزِمْ بِقَوْلِ الغَيْرِ أي: إن يجزم المقلّد بصحة قول الغير جزماً قويًّا؛ بحيث لو رجع - بالفتح - لم يرجع المقلد - بالكسر :وقوله (كَفَى) أي: كفاه في الإيمان، وعلى هذا يحملُ القولُ بكفاية التقليد؛ فيكفيه ذلك في الأحكام الدنيوية؛ فيناكحُ، ويؤمُّ، وتُؤكل ذبيحته، ويرثُه المسلمون ويرتُهم، ويسهم له، ويدفن في مقابر المسلمين ، وفي الأحكام الأخروية أيضاً، فلا يخلد في النار إن دخلها، وماله إلى النجاة والجنة ، فهو مؤمن، لكنه عاص بترك النظر إن كان فيه أهلية للنظر لفظيا ; هو الخلاف الواقع بين من قال بإيمانه ومن قال بكفره ; وأما الخلاف الواقع بين من قال بإيمانه، من كون المقلد مؤمناً عاصياً مطلقاً، أو غير عاص مطلقاً، أو فيه التفصيل، فهو معنوي عند البعض الثاني كما هو معنوي عند الأول. انتهى أجهوري. وقوله : (وَإِلا لَمْ يَزَلْ فِي الضَّيْرِ ) أي : وإن لم يجزم المقلّد بصدق قول الغير جزماً قويًّا ; بأن كان جازماً لكن لو رجع المقلد - بالفتح - لرجعَ المقلّد - بالكسر - لم يزل واقعاً في الضير ; لأنه قابل للشك والتردُّدِ، وعلى هذا يُحمل القولُ بعدم كفاية التقليد . والخلاف إنما هو في المقلد الجازم، وأما الشالك والظان، فمتفق على عدم المقلد إيمانهما، وإن كان كلام المصنف يوهم خلاف المراد. والخلاف في إيمان هو بالنظر لأحكام الآخرة وفيما عند الله ، وأمَّا بالنظر لأحكام الدنيا فيكفي فيها الإقرار فقط ; فمن أقرَّ جرت عليه الأحكام الإسلامية، ولم يُحكم عليه بالكفر، إلا إن اقترن بشيء يقتضي الكفر ; كالسجود لصنم .
He said: If he is certain of the statement of the other, it suffices, and otherwise he continues to be in harm. And his statement: (faqala ... etc.) is coordinated to his statement: 'haqqaqa fihi alkashfa' as a coordination of the detailed to the summary. And his statement: (in yajzim bi qawl al-ghayr) meaning: if the muqallid is certain of the correctness of the statement of the other with a strong certainty, such that if the one imitated (with fatha) were to retract, the muqallid (with kasra) would not retract. And his statement: (kafa) meaning: it suffices him in faith, and upon this is carried the statement about the sufficiency of taqlid. So it suffices him in worldly rulings: he may marry, lead prayer, his sacrificial animal is eaten, Muslims inherit from him and he inherits from them, he receives a share, and he is buried in Muslim cemeteries. And also in afterlife rulings: he will not abide forever in Hell if he enters it, and his outcome is to salvation and Paradise. So he is a believer, but he is disobedient by abandoning reflection if he has capacity for reflection. A verbal [dispute]; it is the disagreement that occurred between those who say he is a believer and those who say he is a disbeliever. As for the disagreement that occurred between those who say he is a believer, concerning whether the muqallid is absolutely a disobedient believer, or absolutely not disobedient, or there is detail, it is a substantive dispute according to the second group just as it is substantive according to the first. End of Ajhuri. And his statement: (wa illa lam yazal fi al-dayr) meaning: if the muqallid is not certain of the truthfulness of the other's statement with strong certainty — if he is certain but if the one imitated (with fatha) were to retract, the muqallid (with kasra) would retract — he continues to be in harm because he is susceptible to doubt and hesitation. And upon this is carried the statement about the insufficiency of taqlid. The disagreement is only about the muqallid who has certainty. As for the hesitant and the one who merely thinks, there is agreement on the insufficiency of the muqallid's faith, even if the author's words give a contrary impression. The disagreement regarding the faith of the muqallid is with regard to afterlife rulings and what is with God. As for worldly rulings, mere verbal declaration suffices. So whoever makes a declaration, Islamic rulings apply to him, and he is not judged as a disbeliever unless accompanied by something that necessitates disbelief, such as prostrating to an idol.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a line of verse in the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid. It defines the condition for taqlid (imitation) to be sufficient for faith. The commentator begins by glossing the phrase "in yajzim bi qawl al-ghayr kafa wa illa lam yazal fi al-dayr". He explains that 'yajzim' means the muqallid (imitator) must have a strong, unwavering certainty (jazzm) in the truth of the statement he follows. This certainty is so firm that even if the person he imitates (al-muqallad, with fatha) were to retract his position, the muqallid (with kasra) would not retract. Next, he explains 'kafa' — such certainty suffices for the muqallid's faith. This holds for both worldly and afterlife rulings: worldly matters like marriage, leading prayer, inheritance, and burial proceed as for any Muslim; in the afterlife, he will not be eternally damned. However, he is still a disobedient sinner for not engaging in personal reflection (nazar) if he is capable. The commentator then notes that the dispute over whether such a muqallid is a believer or a disbeliever is merely verbal (lafzi), while the dispute over whether he is disobedient or not is substantive (ma'nawi). The passage continues with the second half of the verse: 'wa illa lam yazal fi al-dayr'. If the muqallid lacks strong certainty — i.e., his conviction is weak such that he would retract if the imitated person retracted — then he remains in harm (dayr), meaning his faith is insufficient because it is open to doubt and hesitation. This underscores that taqlid without jism does not suffice. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the disagreement about the muqallid's iman concerns his standing before God in the afterlife; for worldly purposes, mere verbal profession (iqrar) suffices unless it is accompanied by an act of disbelief like prostrating to an idol.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
مقلد (muqallid)
جزم (jazzm)
أحكام دنيوية (worldly rulings)
أحكام أخروية (afterlife rulings)
نظر (nazar/reflection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe taqlid is always sufficient for faith regardless of the muqallid's certainty.
Correction: The passage shows that taqlid only suffices when the muqallid has strong, unwavering certainty (jazzm) such that he would not retract even if the imitated authority retracts.

Checkpoint
What condition must be met for taqlid to suffice in faith according to this passage?
A) The muqallid must have strong certainty that he would not retract even if the imitated one retracts.
B) The muqallid must have any level of belief.
C) The muqallid must have personal reflection.
D) The muqallid must be a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in faith only suffices when the muqallid has such strong certainty that he would not retract even if his authority does; without that, he remains in harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow a scholar or teacher on a matter of faith, does your certainty reach the level of jazzm—such that you would not abandon that belief even if they recanted? If not, what harm (dayr) might you be in?
?
Is there any area of your faith where you rely on mere assumption (dhann) rather than certainty? What concrete step will you take today to move from assumption to jazzm?
?

The Definition of Iman and Its Placement in Kalam
Definition of iman as tasdiq and justification for its early placement in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classical theologians defined iman as tasdiq and why they placed its discussion early in the study of kalam.

Core Idea
Iman is tasdiq (affirmation), and its placement in kalam reflects its foundational role.

This lesson introduces the definition of iman as interpreted by the majority of Ash'aris and Maturidis, and explains the methodological debate among theologians about where to discuss iman. The author of the poem and his commentator justify starting with iman because it is a prerequisite for engaging in deeper theological investigations.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation. Notice the key term 'tasdiq' and the mention of dispute regarding utterance.
2
Identify the definition of iman
The verse defines iman as tasdiq. The commentary confirms that the majority of Ash'aris, Maturidis, and some Mu'tazila hold this view.
3
Understand the placement debate
Theologians differed: some delayed discussing iman until after divine matters and prophethood, while others placed it first. The author chose the latter because iman is needed before engaging in other topics.
4
Note the shared interpretation
The passage highlights that defining iman as tasdiq is not exclusive to one school; it is common to Ash'aris, Maturidis, and even some Mu'tazila like al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi.
This corrects a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ تعريف الإيمان وأقسامُهُ] - ١٨ - وَفُسِّرَ الإِيمَانُ بِالنَّصْدِيقِ وَالنُّطْقُ فِيهِ الخُلْفُ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ قوله : (وَفُسِّرَ الإِيمَانُ ... إلخ) لما كان الإيمان والإسلام باعتبار متعلقِ مفهومهما - وهو ما علم من الدين بالضرورة - من مباحث علم الكلام كما يعلم من قوله فيما يأتي: (ومن المعلوم ضرورة جحد)، ذكرهما المتكلمون في علم الكلام، لكن اختلفوا في وضعهما، فَأَخَرَهما قومُ عن الإلهيات والنبوات والسمعيات (۲)، وقدَّمهما آخرون ; لاحتياج الخائض في تلك المباحث إليهما، وقد سلك المصنف هذا الطريق، فلذلك قال : (وفسر الإيمان ... إلى آخره ببناء الفعل للمفعول للعلم بفاعله والأصل: وفسَّرَ جمهورُ الأشاعرة والماتريدية وكذا غيرهم من المعتزلة كالصالحي وابن الراوندي (٣).
And iman is interpreted as tasdiq, and regarding the utterance there is disagreement according to verification. He said: (And iman is interpreted... etc.) Because iman and islam, with respect to the object of their concept—which is what is known of religion by necessity—are among the topics of kalam, as will be known from his saying later: (And from what is known by necessity is denial). The mutakallimun mentioned them in kalam, but they differed in their placement. A group delayed them after the divine matters, prophethoods, and sam'iyyat, and others advanced them due to the need of one who enters into those discussions for them. The author followed this path, therefore he said: (And iman is interpreted... to the end) with the verb in the passive voice for the known agent, and the original: And the majority of Ash'aris and Maturidis, and likewise others from the Mu'tazila such as al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi interpreted.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a definition of iman and a justification for its placement early in the kalam discussion. The author, commenting on his own verse, explains that iman and islam, relating to what is known of religion by necessity, are topics of kalam. The mutakallimun (theologians) differed in where to place them: some delayed them after discussions of divinity, prophethood, and sam'iyyat (audible matters, i.e., eschatology), while others advanced them because the one who engages in those discussions needs them first. The author followed the latter approach. He notes that the verb 'fussira' (interpreted) is in the passive voice, indicating that the agent is known: the majority of Ash'aris, Maturidis, and even some Mu'tazila like al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi interpreted iman as tasdiq (affirmation). This sets up the definition that follows.

Correction: The passage notes that this interpretation is shared by Maturidis and even some Mu'tazila, such as al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi.

The Five Divisions of Iman
Enumeration of five levels of iman with associated spiritual stations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five ascending levels of iman as described by the author, from blind acceptance to the highest spiritual realization.

Core Idea
Faith has degrees from simple acceptance to annihilation in God.

This passage presents a beautiful taxonomy of faith, outlining five levels that a believer can experience. The author begins with the most basic form—faith by blind imitation—and moves upward through knowledge, constant vigilance, direct witnessing, until the final stage where only God is seen. This classification helps us understand the spiritual journey and the different stations of the faithful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the five levels of iman.
Notice the progression from taqlid to haqiqa.
2
Identify each level and its group
For each level, note the definition and the spiritual rank or group of people associated with it (e.g., taqlid for common people).
The levels are: taqlid, 'ilm, 'ayan, haqq, haqiqa.
3
Understand the special mention
Pay attention to the additional 'reality of reality' (haqiqat al-haqiqa) reserved for messengers, which cannot be disclosed.
This shows the limit of human understanding.
4
Reflect on the progression
Consider how each level builds upon the previous one, moving from external authority to direct spiritual experience.
This progression mirrors the spiritual path of the believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الإيمان على خمسة أقسام : إيمان عن تقليد: وهو الإيمانُ الناشئ عن الأخذ بقول الشيخ من غير دليل . وإيمان عن علم: وهو الإيمان الناشئ عن معرفة العقائد بأدلتها . وإيمان عن عيان: وهو الإيمان الناشئ عن عين مراقبة القلب الله ; بحيث لا يغيب عنه طرقةً طرفة عين. وإيمان عن حقٌّ : وهو الإيمانُ الناشئ عن مشاهدة الله بالقلب. وإيمان عن حقيقة : وهو الإيمانُ الناشئ عن كونه لا يشهدُ إلا الله . فالتقليد للعوام، والعلم لأصحاب الأدلة، والعيانُ لأهل المراقبة، ويُسمَّى مقامَ المراقبة، والحقُّ للعارفين، ويُسمَّى مقام المشاهدة، والحقيقة للواقفين، ويُسمّى مقام الفناء; لأنهم يفنّون عن غير الله، ولا يشهدون إلا إيَّاه، وأما حقيقة الحقيقة فهي للمرسلين وقد منعنا الله من كشفها ، فلا سبيل إلى بيانها
And know that iman is of five types: Iman from taqlid: it is iman arising from taking the word of the shaykh without evidence. Iman from knowledge: it is iman arising from knowing the beliefs with their proofs. Iman from direct vision (ayan): it is iman arising from the eye of the heart's vigilance of God, such that he is not absent from Him for the blink of an eye. Iman from truth (haqq): it is iman arising from witnessing God with the heart. Iman from reality (haqiqa): it is iman arising from his not witnessing except God. So taqlid is for the common people, knowledge for the people of proofs, direct vision for the people of vigilance (muraqaba), and it is called the station of vigilance. Truth is for the knowers ('arifun), and it is called the station of witnessing. Reality is for those who stand (waqifun), and it is called the station of annihilation (fana'), because they annihilate from other than God and do not witness except Him. As for the reality of reality (haqiqat al-haqiqa), it is for the messengers, and God has prevented us from uncovering it, so there is no way to explain it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a classification of iman into five ascending levels. The author lists: 1) Iman by taqlid (blind acceptance) from a shaykh without evidence, suitable for the common people. 2) Iman by knowledge, achieved by knowing doctrines with their proofs, for the people of evidence. 3) Iman by direct vision (ayan), arising from constant heart vigilance of God, for the people of muraqaba, called the station of vigilance. 4) Iman by truth (haqq), arising from witnessing God with the heart, for the knowers ('arifun), called the station of witnessing. 5) Iman by reality (haqiqa), where one witnesses nothing but God, for those who stand (waqifun), called the station of annihilation. The author then mentions a further level, the 'reality of reality', which is exclusive to messengers and cannot be disclosed.

The Ruling of Iman During Sleep and Unconsciousness
A note affirming iman's legal continuity during unconsciousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a believer's iman remains legally intact even in states of unconsciousness, and that the rulings of iman continue to apply.

Core Idea
Iman is not interrupted by sleep, madness, or death — it remains legally valid.

This short note addresses a practical question: does a believer's faith count when they are not conscious? The author affirms that iman is a continuous legal status, not dependent on momentary awareness. As we read, we see that the rulings of iman apply without interruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Get a feel for the author's concise style.
2
Notice the list of states
The author lists five states: sleep, heedlessness, insanity, fainting, and death. Observe that these are all states where consciousness is absent.
3
Understand the legal principle
Focus on the key phrase: 'is definitely characterized by iman, legally.' This means iman persists as a legal status, so rulings like inheritance or burial remain valid.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think that iman is suspended during unconsciousness. This passage explicitly corrects that error.
The author references his larger work as authority.

Source Text

Commentary
This short passage is a note (tanbih) on the legal status of a believer in states of unconsciousness. The author asserts that a believer, even when asleep, heedless, insane, fainted, or dead, is still characterized by iman in a legal sense, meaning the rulings of iman continue to apply. This is based on the author's larger commentary. The passage guards against the misconception that iman is temporarily suspended when a person is not conscious.

The Meaning of Tasdiq in Iman: Submission, Not Just Assent
The author defines tasdiq and distinguishes it from mere mental assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true faith (iman) requires tasdiq as heartfelt submission and acceptance, not merely intellectual acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Faith is not just knowing the truth but submitting to it with your heart.

This passage from the commentary on the Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a critical nuance in defining iman. The author explains that the tasdiq (affirmation) in the definition of faith is not a cold intellectual assent but an active submission (idhan) and acceptance (qabul) of what the Prophet brought. This distinction guards against a superficial understanding of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms: تصديق, إذعان, قبول.
The passage is incomplete but contains the core idea.
2
Identify the two components of tasdiq
The author explicitly states that tasdiq means 'submission (idhan) to what he brought and acceptance (qabul) of it'. Find these two terms and understand their significance.
Contrast with the phrase 'attribution of truthfulness' which alone is insufficient.
3
Understand why this distinction matters
Ask yourself: what difference does it make if tasdiq is just mental assent versus active submission? The author warns that without submission, one is not a believer.
This connects to the broader doctrine of iman in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِالتَّصْدِيقِ) أي: التصديق المعهودِ شرعاً (۳) ، وهو تصديق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في كلِّ ما جاء به وعُلمَ من الدين بالضرورة ) ؛ أي: عُلم من أدلَّة الدين بشبه الضرورة، فهو نظري في الأصل (٥) ، إلا أنه لما اشتهر صارَ ملحقاً بالضروري بجامع الجزم في كلِّ من العام والخاص من غير قبول للتشكيك (٦) . والمراد بتصديق النبي في ذلك : الإذعان لما جاء به والقبول له، وليس المراد وقوع نسبة الصدق إليه في القلب من غير إذعان وقبول له حتى يلزم الحكم بإيمان
His saying: (by tasdiq) means: the tasdiq known in divine law, and it is tasdiq of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in everything he brought and that is known of religion by necessity; i.e., known from the proofs of religion with quasi-necessity, for it is theoretical in origin, except that when it became widespread it became attached to the necessary by virtue of certainty in both general and specific, without acceptance of doubt. And the intended meaning of tasdiq of the Prophet in that is: submission (idhan) to what he brought and acceptance (qabul) of it, and it is not intended that the attribution of truthfulness to him occurs in the heart without submission and acceptance, so that the judgment of iman would be binding.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the term 'tasdiq' (affirmation) in the definition of iman. The author clarifies that tasdiq here is the legally recognized affirmation: attesting to the Prophet in everything he brought that is known of religion by necessity. This knowledge is actually theoretical in origin, but due to its widespread fame, it has become like necessary knowledge, being certain for both the elite and commoner without admitting doubt. The author further emphasizes that true tasdiq is not mere mental acknowledgment of the Prophet's truthfulness, but rather active submission (idhan) and acceptance (qabul) of what he brought. Without this submission, one would not be considered a believer. The passage cuts off, but the argument is that iman requires heartfelt submission, not just cognitive assent.

Correction: The author insists that tasdiq requires active submission and acceptance of the message, not just mental acknowledgment.

Tasdiq in iman is active submission and acceptance of the Prophet's message, not mere mental assent.

The Five Levels of Faith: From Taqlid to Haqiqah
Sanusi classifies iman into five ascending levels of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five degrees of iman (faith) as classified by spiritual certainty, from simple imitation to direct witnessing and annihilation in Allah.

Core Idea
Faith has degrees: from blind imitation to realizing nothing exists but Allah.

In Islamic spirituality, faith is not a single state but a journey of increasing certainty. This passage from the Jawharah presents a classic classification of five levels of iman, each corresponding to a different spiritual station. As you read, notice how each level is defined by the believer's relationship with Allah—from forgetfulness to constant awareness to utter absorption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the sequence: taqlid, ilm, iyan, haqq, haqiqah.
2
Identify the five types
The passage presents five types of iman. Find each one and note its definition: what does it arise from and who is associated with it?
Taqlid and ilm are mentioned only in the assignment part.
3
Understand the stations
Each type corresponds to a spiritual station: murāqabah, mushāhadah, fanā’. Reflect on how these stations describe closeness to Allah.
The highest mentioned is haqiqat al-haqiqah, reserved for messengers.
4
Ask: why does this matter?
Ask yourself why distinguishing levels of faith is important. This classification encourages spiritual growth and shows that faith can deepen.
This connects to the learning objective about degrees of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإيمان عن عيان: وهو الإيمان الناشئ عن عين مراقبة القلب الله؛ بحيث لا يغيب عنه طرقةً طرفة عين. وإيمان عن حقٌّ: وهو الإيمانُ الناشئ عن مشاهدة الله بالقلب. وإيمان عن حقيقة: وهو الإيمانُ الناشئ عن كونه لا يشهدُ إلا الله. فالتقليد للعوام، والعلم لأصحاب الأدلة، والعيانُ لأهل المراقبة، ويُسمَّى مقامَ المراقبة، والحقُّ للعارفين، ويُسمَّى مقام المشاهدة، والحقيقة للواقفين، ويُسمّى مقام الفناء؛ لأنهم يفنّون عن غير الله، ولا يشهدون إلا إيَّاه، وأما حقيقة الحقيقة فهي للمرسلين وقد منعنا الله من كشفها، فلا سبيل إلى بيانها.
And iman from vision: it is iman arising from the eye of the heart's watchfulness of Allah; such that he is not absent from Him for the blink of an eye. And iman from truth: it is iman arising from witnessing Allah with the heart. And iman from reality: it is iman arising from his not witnessing except Allah. So taqlid is for the common people, knowledge is for the people of proofs, vision is for the people of watchfulness, and it is called the station of watchfulness, and truth is for the knowers (al-ʿārifīn), and it is called the station of witnessing, and reality is for the stationers (al-wāqifīn), and it is called the station of annihilation (al-fanāʾ); because they annihilate from other than Allah, and they do not witness except Him. But the reality of reality is for the messengers, and Allah has prevented us from uncovering it, so there is no way to explain it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a classification of iman (faith) according to its degree of certainty and spiritual attainment. The author describes five types: iman al-ʿiyān (faith from vision), iman al-ḥaqq (faith from truth), and iman al-ḥaqīqah (faith from reality), preceded in context by taqlīd and ʿilm (knowledge) which are mentioned later. The passage explains each:
- Iman al-ʿiyān arises from the heart's constant watchfulness of Allah (murāqabah), such that the believer never forgets Him for an instant.
- Iman al-ḥaqq arises from witnessing Allah with the heart (mushāhadah).
- Iman al-ḥaqīqah arises from seeing nothing but Allah, i.e., complete annihilation of other-than-Allah from one's awareness.
The author then assigns each level to a specific group: taqlīd is for the common people (ʿawāmm), ʿilm for the people of proofs (aṣḥāb al-adillah), ʿiyān for the people of watchfulness (ahl al-murāqabah) which is called the station of murāqabah, ḥaqq for the knowers (al-ʿārifīn) which is the station of mushāhadah, and ḥaqīqah for the stationers (al-wāqifīn) which is the station of fanāʾ (annihilation). The highest level, ḥaqīqat al-ḥaqīqah (reality of reality), is exclusively for the messengers and cannot be disclosed.

Key Terms
إيمان عن عيان
إيمان عن حق
إيمان عن حقيقة

علم
مراقبة
مشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All iman is identical; there is no distinction in degrees of faith.
Correction: This passage shows that iman has multiple degrees, from simple acceptance (taqlid) to direct perception (haqiqah), with higher levels requiring spiritual purification.

Checkpoint

A) Iman al-ʿiyān
B) Iman al-ḥaqq
C) Iman al-ḥaqīqah
D) Taqlīd

Key Takeaway
Iman is not uniform; it deepens through spiritual practice from mere acceptance to direct perception of Allah, culminating in a state that remains beyond description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are you willing to pursue the highest level accessible to you, even if you know that the ultimate reality is beyond your grasp? What would that pursuit look like starting tonight?

Tasdiq: Affirmation with Submission
Defines shar'i tasdiq as affirmation of the Prophet with submission, distinguishing it from mere intellectual assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true tasdiq (affirmation) in the shar'i sense requires both intellectual assent and active submission to the Prophet's message.

Core Idea
Faith is not just knowing truths; it is submitting to them.

The text of the Jawharah defines tasdiq as the core of iman. But what exactly does tasdiq mean? The commentator clarifies that it is not mere mental acknowledgment. It is a comprehensive affirmation that includes submission and acceptance of the Prophet's message. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Notice how the author distinguishes between types of knowledge (necessary, theoretical, quasi-necessary).
The term 'shibh al-ḍarūrah' is key.
2
Identify the two components of tasdiq
The passage says tasdiq is 'submission (idhʿān) and acceptance (qubūl)' toward the Prophet's message. Underline these terms.
3
Contrast with mere mental assent
The author explicitly excludes 'just attributing truthfulness in the heart' without submission. Why? Because faith must transform the whole person.
This guards against a purely intellectual view of iman.
4
Reflect on the nature of religious knowledge
The knowledge that leads to tasdiq is originally theoretical but becomes quasi-necessary due to its certainty. How does that apply to your own conviction?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (بالتَّصْدِيقِ) أي: التصديق المعهودِ شرعاً، وهو تصديق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في كلِّ ما جاء به وعُلمَ من الدين بالضرورة؛ أي: عُلم من أدلَّة الدين بشبه الضرورة، فهو نظري في الأصل، إلا أنه لما اشتهر صارَ ملحقاً بالضروري بجامع الجزم في كلِّ من العام والخاص من غير قبول للتشكيك. والمراد بتصديق النبي في ذلك: الإذعان لما جاء به والقبول له، وليس المراد وقوع نسبة الصدق إليه في القلب من غير إذعان وقبول له حتى يلزم الحكم بإيمان.
His saying: (by al-taṣdīq) meaning: the well-known shar'i affirmation, and it is affirming the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in everything he brought and is known from religion necessarily; i.e., it is known from the proofs of religion quasi-necessarily, so it is theoretical in origin, but when it became widespread it became annexed to the necessary by virtue of certainty in both the general and the particular without accepting doubt. And what is meant by affirming the Prophet in that is: submission (al-idhʿān) to what he brought and acceptance of him, and it is not meant the occurrence of attributing truthfulness to him in the heart without submission and acceptance for him so that the ruling of iman would be required.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'tasdiq' as used in the text of the Jawharah. The author explains that 'tasdiq' here refers to the well-known shar'i affirmation, which is affirming the Prophet in everything he brought and that is known from the religion necessarily (bi al-ḍarūrah). He clarifies that this knowledge, though originally theoretical (naẓarī), has become so widespread that it is annexed to necessary knowledge in terms of certainty, both for the general and the learned. Then he distinguishes tasdiq from mere intellectual assent: true tasdiq includes submission (idhʿān) and acceptance (qubūl). If a person merely acknowledges the Prophet's truthfulness in his heart without submission, that is not sufficient for iman. The passage thus guards against a reductionist understanding of faith as only cognitive belief.

القبول
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think faith is just believing facts about the Prophet in one's mind.
Correction: The passage requires active submission and acceptance; mental assent alone does not constitute iman.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does shar'i tasdiq include beyond mere belief?
A) Memorization of Hadith
B) Submission and acceptance
C) Performing rituals
D) Public declaration

Key Takeaway
True faith is a wholehearted submission to the Prophet's message, not just intellectual agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear a command from the Qur'an or Sunnah that conflicts with your desires, do you internally negotiate or do you submit with acceptance? Which response builds true iman?
?
Is your faith a collection of beliefs you hold, or is it a lived submission that shapes your choices? Today, choose one teaching you intellectually accept but have not submitted to, and take a concrete step toward surrender.

Ignorance as an Excuse Before Proof
The author explains and applies the principle that ignorance excuses before proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ignorance is a valid excuse before the divine proof is established, and that disbelief requires knowledge and willful rejection.

Core Idea
Ignorance excuses before proof; obstinacy after teaching is disbelief.

This passage from the commentary establishes a key principle in Islamic theology: one cannot be held accountable for disbelief in matters not accessible to reason until the proof is conveyed. The author then applies this to the knowledge of prophets, using the example of al-Yasa' (Elisha) to illustrate that ignorance of his name and message is not disbelief unless accompanied by obstinacy after being taught.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author begins with a general principle and then gives a specific example.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يُعدُّ كفراً إلا بعناد بعد تعليم' (is not considered disbelief except out of obstinacy after teaching).
2
Understand the principle
The passage states that knowledge of divine attributes is not attained by reason; therefore, before proof, ignorance excuses. Reflect on why this matters for judging disbelief.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the case of al-Yasa'. Why does the author use him? How does this example reinforce the principle?
The example shows that even for widely known prophets, ignorance excuses before teaching.
4
Identify the condition for disbelief
From the passage, disbelief only occurs with 'obstinacy after teaching' (عناد بعد تعليم). Discuss what this means for accountability.
This is the key takeaway: disbelief requires knowledge and willful rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة فإنه يُعذر بالجهل ؛ لأن علم ذلك لا يدرك بالعقل ولا الروية والفكر، فتُثبت هذه الصفات وتنفي عنه التشبيه كما نفى عن نفسه فقال : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْ) [الشورى: ۱۱])، فاليسع عليه السلام مثلاً أكثرُ العامة يجهلون اسمه فضلاً عن رسالته، فالظاهر أنه كغيره من المتواتر، لا يُعدُّ كفراً إلا بعناد بعد تعليم . أمير (ص ٥٧).
The proof: he is excused by ignorance, because knowledge of that is not attained by reason, reflection, or thought. So these attributes are affirmed and anthropomorphism is negated from Him, as He negated it from Himself when He said: 'There is nothing like unto Him' [Al-Shura 42:11]. So al-Yasa' (peace be upon him), for example, most of the common people are ignorant of his name, let alone his message. So the apparent view is that he, like other widely known matters, is not considered disbelief except out of obstinacy after teaching. Amir (p. 57).
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a proof and application of the principle that ignorance is an excuse before the proof (hujjah) is established. The author argues that knowledge of divine attributes is not accessible by reason alone, so one who denies them out of ignorance is excused. The passage then applies the same principle to prophets: using the example of al-Yasa' (Elisha), it notes that many people do not even know his name, so denying him cannot be considered disbelief unless the person does so obstinately after having been taught. This establishes that disbelief requires knowledge and willful rejection.

Correction: The passage specifies that ignorance excuses only before the proof is established; after the proof, obstinate denial is disbelief.

C) He is excused but must learn.
D) He is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance excuses disbelief only before the proof is established; after that, obstinate rejection constitutes disbelief.

Uttering the Shahada: Ruling for the Mute and Children of Muslims
Explains rulings on uttering shahada for different people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rulings regarding the utterance of the shahada for the capable, the mute, and children of Muslims, as well as the specific wording required.

Core Idea
Utterance of the shahada is required for the capable, but exceptions apply to the mute and Muslim children.

This passage from the commentary on the Jawharah addresses a key practical question: Is uttering the shahada necessary for faith? The author explains the scholarly disagreement, clarifies who is exempt, and details the exact wording needed. This lesson will help you navigate the nuances of this foundational act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's style and the flow of rulings.
2
Identify the three categories discussed
The passage covers three groups: the capable person, the mute, and the children of Muslims. Note the distinct ruling for each.
The capable person is required to utter; the mute is not; children of Muslims are believers regardless.
3
Understand the disagreement on utterance
The passage mentions that there is scholarly disagreement on whether utterance is integral to iman. This sets the context for the rules that follow.
The disagreement is specifically about an original disbeliever entering Islam.
4
Note the requirement of 'Ashhadu'
The passage states that the word 'Ashhadu' is necessary and must be repeated, but the conjunction ('and') is not required.
Al-Ziyadi and later al-Ramli held that 'Ashhadu' is not strictly required, but the passage indicates the dominant view.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these rulings apply in practice: a mute person is considered a believer without utterance; a Muslim child is a believer even if they never speak the shahada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ النطق بالشهادتين وحكمُ الأخرس وأولاد المسلمين] قوله : (وَالنُّطْقُ فِيهِ الخُلْفُ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ) أي : وفي النطق بالشهادتين للمتمكن منه وهو القادرُ عليه - في جهة اعتبار مدخليَّتِهِ في الإيمان، الاختلاف بين العلماء، وسيأتي تفصيله عقبه، فحذف المصنف المنطوق به ؛ وهو قوله : أشهدُ أن لا إلهَ إِلَّا اللهُ وأشهدُ أنَّ محمداً رسولُ اللهِ، كما سيصرح به في قوله : وجامع معنى الذي تقرّرا شهادنا الإسلام فاطرح المرا) وخرج بالمتمكّن ـ الذي هو القادر - الأخرسُ ، فلا يُطالب بالنطقِ ؛ كَمَنِ اخترمَتْهُ المنية قبل النطق به من غير تراخ، فهو مؤمن عند الله حتى على القول بأنَّ النطق شرط صحةٍ أو شطر، بخلاف مَنْ تمكَّنَ وفَرَّطَ . وموضوع هذا الخلاف : كافر أصلي يريد الدخول في الإسلام. وأما أولاد المسلمين فمؤمنون قطعاً، وتجري عليهم الأحكام الدنيوية ولو لم ينطقوا بالشهادتين طول عمرِهِمْ . ولا بدَّ من لفظ (أشهد ) وتكريره، ولا يشترط أن يأتي بحرف العطف على ما قاله الزيادي، ورجع إليه الرملي آخر (۱) ، فلا يكفي إبدال لفظ (أشهد) بغيره وإن كان ... (1) سيأتي قريباً قول الرملي في هذه المسألة، وعبارة الشرواني في حاشيته على التحفة» (٤٧١/١): (وقال شيخنا الزيادي : إن الشيخ - يعني الرملي - رجع إليه آخراً، وعبارة العلقمي عند قوله : أسعد الناس بشفاعتي يوم القيامة من قال : لا إله إلا الله مخلصاً من قلبه نصها : ومنه يؤخذ أنه لا يشترط في التلفظ عند الإسلام بكلمة الشهادة أن يقول : أشهد وهو الراجح المعتمد، بل هو الصواب، ولا يغتر بما ذكره بعض أهل العصر وأفتى به أنه لا بدَّ من لفظ أشهد)، ومما يفهم عدم اشتراط لفظ الشهادتين قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لم يقل يوماً : رب اغفر لي خطيئتي يوم الدين» في حق ابن جدعان، وانظر المفهم على مسلم (٨٦/٣) .
[The utterance of the two testimonies (shahadas) and the ruling of the mute and the children of Muslims.] His saying: 'And regarding utterance there is disagreement upon verification' meaning: regarding the utterance of the two shahadas for one capable of it — that is, the one able to do it — concerning consideration of its integrality in iman, there is disagreement among the scholars, and its detail will come after it. So the author omitted the uttered words, i.e., his saying: 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah,' as he will explicitly state in his saying: 'And the collector of the meaning that was settled, our two shahadas of Islam, so put aside doubt.' And he excluded by 'one capable' — which is the able — the mute; so he is not required to utter, like one whom death overtakes before uttering without delay, so he is a believer before Allah even on the view that utterance is a condition of validity or a part, unlike one who was able and neglected. The subject of this disagreement: an original disbeliever who wants to enter Islam. As for the children of Muslims, they are definitively believers, and the worldly rulings apply to them even if they never utter the two shahadas throughout their lives. And the wording 'I bear witness (Ashhadu)' is necessary along with its repetition, and it is not required that he brings the conjunction according to what al-Ziyadi said, and al-Ramli later reverted to him (1). (1) The saying of al-Ramli on this matter will come shortly, and the expression of al-Shirwani in his Hashiyah upon al-Tuhfah (1/471): 'Our shaykh al-Ziyadi said: that the shaykh — meaning al-Ramli — reverted to him later, and the expression of al-'Alqami when he said: 'The most fortunate of people in my intercession on the Day of Resurrection is the one who says 'La ilaha illa Allah' sincerely from his heart,' its text: 'And from it is taken that it is not required in uttering at the time of Islam the word of shahada that he say 'Ashhadu', and that is the preponderant and relied-upon view, rather it is correct, and do not be deceived by what some modern scholars mentioned and issued fatwa that 'Ashhadu' is necessary.' And from what indicates the non-requirement of the wording of the two shahadas is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'He did not say one day: O Lord, forgive my sin on the Day of Judgment' concerning Ibn Jud'an. And refer to al-Mufhim on Muslim (3/86).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses the requirement of uttering the two shahadas (Ashhadu an la ilaha illa Allah, Ashhadu anna Muhammadan rasul Allah) in relation to faith. It notes that there is scholarly disagreement on whether utterance is integral to iman. The commentator clarifies that for a capable person (the one who can speak), the utterance is required; but the mute is exempted. Similarly, one who dies before having a chance to utter is still a believer in Allah's sight, even on the view that utterance is a condition. The subject of this disagreement is specifically an original disbeliever entering Islam. Children of Muslims are believers unquestionably, regardless of utterance. Then the passage discusses the wording: the word 'Ashhadu' must be said and repeated, but according to al-Ziyadi and later al-Ramli, it is not necessary to bring the conjunction (i.e., the 'and' between the two parts). A footnote cites al-Shirwani's Hashiyah, supporting that the phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone may suffice, and warns against the fatwa requiring 'Ashhadu' as being incorrect. The mention of Ibn Jud'an in the footnote suggests that even a non-Muslim who said something good is not necessarily a believer, but that is used to support the non-requirement of a specific wording.

الأخرس
أولاد المسلمين
أشهد
الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That uttering the shahada is an absolute requirement for being considered a Muslim, with no exceptions.
Correction: This passage shows that the mute and children of Muslims are exceptions, and there is a scholarly disagreement on whether utterance is integral to iman.

Checkpoint

B) He is not required to utter it

Key Takeaway
Utterance of the shahada is required for the capable, but the mute and children of Muslims are exceptions and are considered believers without it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely said the shahada countless times. But when you say 'Ashhadu'—do you truly feel the weight of bearing witness, as if standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment? What concrete change will you make today to ensure your next utterance is from the depths of your heart?

The mute person is excused from uttering the shahada, yet is still considered a believer. Does your confidence in your own faith rest on your tongue or on the reality in your soul? What would be left of your iman if you could never speak it again?

Definitions of Faith: Linguistic and Legal
The author defines faith linguistically and legally with a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the linguistic and legal definitions of faith (iman) and why the legal definition adds a necessary condition.

Core Idea
Faith is not just any belief — it must be attested to everything brought by the Prophet.

In this passage, the author clarifies that faith has two meanings: one in the Arabic language (absolute attestation) and one in Islamic law (attestation to all that is necessarily known from the Prophet). Understanding this difference is essential for correct theology, as it prevents reducing faith to mere linguistic trust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from the comparison to the two definitions.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse quoted.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the author defines faith legally and linguistically. Underline the key phrases: 'الإيمان شرعاً' and 'وأما لغة'.
The legal definition includes conditions that the linguistic one does not.
3
Understand the comparison
The opening sentence compares detailed and summary knowledge. Ask yourself: why does the author mention this? How does it relate to faith?
This sets up that faith can be held in detail or summary, but both are valid.
4
Learn from the Quranic proof
The verse from Surah Yusuf shows that 'mu'min' in language means 'attester'. Recognize how the author uses scripture to support the linguistic definition.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about the difference: linguistic faith is just any attestation, but legal faith requires attesting to all that is necessarily known from the Prophet. Why is this important?
This guards against incomplete faith.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتفصيلي أكمل من الإجمالي من حيث التفصيلُ، وإلا فهو مثله من حيث الخروج من عُهدة التكليف بكل منهما . وبالجملة: فالإيمان شرعاً : هو التصديق بجميع ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ممَّا عُلم من الدين بالضرورة إجمالاً في الإجمالي، وتفصيلاً في التفصيلي، وأما لغة: فهو مطلق التصديق، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَنتَ بِمُؤْمِنِ لَنَا) [يوسف: ١٧) أي : بمصدق .
And the detailed is more complete than the summary in terms of detail, otherwise it is like it in terms of fulfilling the obligation of commissioning with each of them. In summary: faith legally is the attestation to everything brought by the Prophet (peace be upon him) from what is known of the religion by necessity, summarily in the summary and in detail in the detailed. As for linguistically, it is absolute attestation, and from it is His saying: 'And you are not a believer for us' [Yusuf: 17], i.e., attester.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines faith (iman) by distinguishing its legal and linguistic meanings. It begins by comparing detailed and summary knowledge, stating that the detailed is more complete in detail but equal in fulfilling obligation. Then it gives the legal (shar'i) definition: faith is attestation (tasdiq) to everything the Prophet brought that is known necessarily from religion, either summarily or in detail. The linguistic definition is simply absolute attestation, supported by Quran 12:17 where 'mu'min' means 'attester'.

Correction: The passage clarifies that faith in the Islamic legal sense is specifically attesting to everything brought by the Prophet that is known necessarily, not just any belief or trust.

The Dispute Over the Utterance of the Two Testimonies
The author explains the scholarly dispute about utterance of the two testimonies and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly dispute about whether uttering the two testimonies is integral to faith, and the rulings for the mute, the dying, and the children of Muslims.

Core Idea
Utterance of the two testimonies is disputed for converts but not required for the mute or Muslim children.

The poem's line 'والنطق فيه الخلف بالتحقيق' alerts us to a key disagreement among scholars: Is saying the two testimonies a part of faith itself? This passage from the commentary clarifies the scope of that dispute. It explains who is required to utter, who is excused, and who is automatically considered a believer regardless of utterance. By examining these distinctions, we gain a precise understanding of the relationship between inner faith and outward speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central dispute
The passage begins by stating there is a dispute among scholars about whether utterance of the two testimonies is a part of faith. Note that the author says the detail will come later.
The dispute is only for the one who is capable, 'al-mutamakkin'.
2
Understand the exception for the mute
The passage explicitly excludes the mute (al-akhras) from the requirement to utter. Also mentioned is one who dies before having a chance to utter. Both are considered believers even if they never spoke the testimony.
This holds even on the view that utterance is a condition or a part.
3
Recognize the subject of the dispute
The dispute applies specifically to an original unbeliever who wants to enter Islam. This is the context in which scholars differ about the necessity of outward articulation.
For someone who is able but neglected, the ruling is different.
4
Learn the special status of children of Muslims
The passage concludes that the children of Muslims are definitively believers, and worldly rulings apply to them even if they never utter the testimonies their whole lives.
This clarifies that Muslim children do not need to articulate faith for Islamic rulings to apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالنُّطْقُ فِيهِ الخُلْفُ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ) أي : وفي النطق بالشهادتين للمتمكن منه وهو القادرُ عليه - في جهة اعتبار مدخليَّتِهِ في الإيمان، الاختلاف بين العلماء، وسيأتي تفصيله عقبه، فحذف المصنف المنطوق به ؛ وهو قوله : أشهدُ أن لا إلهَ إِلَّا اللهُ وأشهدُ أنَّ محمداً رسولُ اللهِ، كما سيصرح به في قوله : وجامع معنى الذي تقرّرا شهادنا الإسلام فاطرح المرا) وخرج بالمتمكّن ـ الذي هو القادر - الأخرسُ ، فلا يُطالب بالنطقِ ؛ كَمَنِ اخترمَتْهُ المنية قبل النطق به من غير تراخ، فهو مؤمن عند الله حتى على القول بأنَّ النطق شرط صحةٍ أو شطر، بخلاف مَنْ تمكَّنَ وفَرَّطَ . وموضوع هذا الخلاف : كافر أصلي يريد الدخول في الإسلام. وأما أولاد المسلمين فمؤمنون قطعاً، وتجري عليهم الأحكام الدنيوية ولو لم ينطقوا بالشهادتين طول عمرِهِمْ .
His saying: 'And the utterance in it is a dispute with verification' i.e., concerning the utterance of the two testimonies for the one who is able and capable—regarding the consideration of its entering into faith, there is disagreement among scholars, and its detail will come after it. So the author omitted the uttered thing, which is his saying: 'I bear witness that there is no god but Allah and I bear witness that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', as he will clarify in his saying: 'And the one who includes the meaning which has been established, our two testimonies of Islam, so leave off contention.' And by 'al-mutamakkin' (the capable one) he excludes the mute, so he is not required to utter; like one whom death overtakes before uttering without delay, then he is a believer before Allah even on the view that utterance is a condition of validity or a part, unlike the one who was capable and neglected. The subject of this dispute is an original unbeliever who wants to enter Islam. As for the children of Muslims, they are believers definitively, and the worldly rulings apply to them even if they do not utter the two testimonies throughout their lives.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage explains the line 'والنطق فيه الخلف بالتحقيق' from the poem. It identifies the dispute (khilaf) about the utterance (nutq) of the two testimonies—whether it is integral to faith. The author clarifies that the dispute applies to the capable person (al-mutamakkin), not the mute (al-akhras). The mute is not required to utter; likewise one who dies before having a chance is a believer even on the view that utterance is a condition or part. The dispute concerns an original unbeliever converting to Islam. The children of Muslims are believers definitively regardless of uttering the testimonies.

Key Terms

الشهادتين
الأخرس
أولاد المسلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Utterance of the two testimonies is mandatory for everyone, including the mute and children of Muslims.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the mute is not required to utter, and children of Muslims are considered believers even if they never utter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is not required to utter the two testimonies?
A) The original unbeliever
B) The capable Muslim
C) The mute and the children of Muslims
D) Only the insane

Key Takeaway
The utterance of the two testimonies is subject to scholarly dispute for converts, but it is not required for the mute, the one who dies before opportunity, or children of Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the ease given to the mute and children of Muslims, do you feel gratitude for your own ability to utter the testimonies? Have you ever taken that ability for granted? What would it mean to you if you were suddenly unable to speak the shahada?
?
The passage emphasizes that the mute is not required to utter, yet he is a believer. How does this challenge your understanding of the relationship between actions and faith? Are there areas in your life where you rely on external practices while neglecting inner conviction?
?
For those who are capable, the utterance is a subject of dispute. Yet, how many times have you spoken the shahada without reflecting on its weight? Commit today to uttering the two testimonies with full presence of heart at least once a day.
